{"uri": "2024-07-410025367", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:36:32", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:36:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://scnow.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_ad9f9b2d-9d67-5b18-8f69-039ac78dd2ae.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "By BILL BARROW - Associated Press\n\nATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nFlorence's Red Bone Alley changes hands Florence's Seminar brewing sold to Victor's, Notch 8 owners Friday Pamplico crash claims life of car's driver Florence woman dies in Thursday afternoon Gilbert Drive fire Former Sonoco president, civic leader Charles Coker dies One person dies, other injured in 'accidental' shooting Shemar Simes back to slugging ways for Florence Post 1 American Airline adds another flight to Florence Two arrested, charged in Howe Springs fatal hit-and-un Lancaster man charged in Sunday Florence shooting Post 1 wins again, but issues with walks & strikeouts remain Darlington free clinic honors four of its founders Victory Sports Camp approaching 15 years of service in Mullins Flying umbrella impales woman visiting beach with her family, officials say Tuten, staff pushing right buttons for Flamingos thus far\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates\n\nThat old age question (again)\n\nGaffes galore\n\nGetting personal\n\nBlunders without words\n\nBiden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "2024-07-410031524", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:41:04", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:41:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://nptelegraph.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_7de69f73-62bb-5033-a00b-d8fbe9b715f6.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "Democratic Party leaders are publicly standing by President Joe Biden after a disjointed debate performance\n\nATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nNorth Platte School Board continues debate on teacher dress code Man taken into custody near Brady after pursuit, manhunt Saturday More businesses inevitable on road to Maloney, Planning Commission tells boat-shop foes Kayaker says Nebraska a 'water wonderland' filled with hidden gems Retired Paxton village clerk pursues her 'passion' with City Bakery Missouri church announcement called for young men to form local militia. It was a mistake, pastor says Perkins, the aging diner chain, has a 'new vibe' with a different name K9 unit used to track, arrest man near Brady after Saturday pursuit, manhunt Man wanted on California homicide warrant arrested in Ogallala Walmart parking lot Hispanic residents account for over 80% of Nebraska population growth 2024 British Open odds: Favorites, long shot picks for Open Championship at Royal Troon Several homes damaged, person injured as tornado touches down in Whitman Softball-sized hail reported in Dawson County in severe thunderstorm early Saturday Crete, Nebraska: 7 shot in 'welcoming community' by neighbor who 'hated everybody' Martin Mull, hip comic and actor from 'Fernwood Tonight' and 'Roseanne,' dies at 80\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates\n\nThat old age question (again)\n\nGaffes galore\n\nGetting personal\n\nBlunders without words\n\nBiden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207784105", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:36:54", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:36:54Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:36:15Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://qctimes.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_e3f2bee8-5768-50e9-8f2d-130678395c58.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Moline Post Office supervisor charged for allegedly stealing mail containing gift cards, cash John Deere announces 600+ layoffs at East Moline, Davenport and Dubuque Sarah Goodwin says her husband's life was 'swirling out of control' before he was killed JDC: Two top players out of PGA Tour field PV standout and Cross-Fit athlete named 2024 Beat the Elite Runners Quad-Cities prepare for 18-foot Mississippi River flood Iowa restaurant that wanted to help teens fined for federal labor law violations Quad-Cities business openings, closings: Octeen Energy, Smith's Custom Meats August rap show at The Mark to feature Bone Thugs-N-Harmony, Xzibit and Yung Joc 'There is quiet where he used to be.' Family tells of John Adam Moo, 10, who drowned Genesis, United Healthcare come to agreement Rock Island-Milan schools to get weapons detectors, other security updates The start of soft serve: How an East Moline company has been part of ice cream history for 78 years CPKC train derails in Muscatine County Davenport opens Dohse Splash and Play Park in place of former pool\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207790169", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:40:20", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:40:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:40:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://richmond.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_4c86f4bb-c878-54cf-8031-c4e00b3d4c1a.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Stella Dikos, namesake of Stella's restaurants and markets, dies at age 82 Youngkin congratulates McGuire, says he won 5th District primary Richmond city spokeswoman resigns; was subject of purchasing card questions, FOIA whistleblower lawsuit Meet the 2024 All-Metro baseball team, led by Benedictine's Jake Wise Richmond-area Fourth of July guide: Where to catch the fireworks 7 more arrested in prostitution sting at Chesterfield massage parlors Woman dead after jumping from bridge overlooking I-64 in Henrico Former Virginia EMS employee accused of embezzling $4 million As chief of staff departs, Youngkin tries to reset relationship with assembly Ronnie's BBQ restaurant wins 'Best Chef' at meeting planners convention State employees, teachers due raises as new budget takes effect New restaurant Sweet P's headed for downtown Richmond Williams: Rewrite MLK? The Youngkin administration can't be trusted with history. UPDATE: Suspect arrested in fatal stabbing of Chesterfield woman Richmond city spokesperson had city-issued credit card suspended\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Blunders without words Getting personal Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux Gaffes galore\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207812840", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:54:05", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:54:05Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:53:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8980392217636108, "url": "https://magicvalley.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_6acb0652-feb6-56bd-aff0-72f97092f21a.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Man's body recovered from Snake River near Centennial Park P&Z shoots down In-N-Out's Twin Falls proposal 1 dies after head-on I-84 crash near Wendell Serving the people: Judge Campbell steps down from the bench Death Notices Death Notices Police: Man pointed weapon at motorist in Twin Falls road-rage incident Mormon crickets' cyclical visit stains roads red in southern Idaho Death Notices Idaho Bowl aims to 'elevate football throughout the state' Idaho native Cutler takes executive editor job at Times-News Police: Twin Falls driver hits semi, dies in U.S. 93 crash Parties work on case where car was found with $1 million in drugs Gooding's Colston Loveland named Walter Camp Preseason All-American Death Notices\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207790770", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:47:04", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:47:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:40:24Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8980392217636108, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_ccbb44e4-95f6-5645-96b0-f36da4393fc3.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Okoboji lakes remain at near record levels; water activities still curtailed as July 4 holiday nears Panel looks to release more water from Okoboji lakes without hurting flood-stricken Spencer, Cherokee Sergeant Bluff-Luton assistant principal, AD resigns days after being placed on administrative leave Where to go for firework displays in Siouxland McCook Lake faces questions of flood recovery, future mitigation Dairy plant sues Sanborn over loss of $4 million in cheese What to know before you go to Saturday in the Park 2024 Siouxland homes without flood insurance face coverage limits and costly repairs after flooding McCook Lake residents voice their flooding concerns to Sen. Mike Rounds OUR OPINION: Noem erred by not having National Guard help with S.D. flooding Cherokee residents try to stave off despair by jumping into action after flood Rock Valley police chief confirms flood-related death Man accused of vandalizing 12 vehicles in Sioux City parking ramp Million gallons of sewage flowing into Missouri River per day from Sioux City wastewater plant High school baseball top performers: East's Colling, RSM's Loucks, MOC's McCarty drive teams' push\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux That old age question (again)\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "2024-07-410038533", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:45:47", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:45:47Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8941176533699036, "url": "https://pressofatlanticcity.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_0f848922-df52-5661-9916-8550a917a95a.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "Some elected Democrats loyal to President Joe Biden raised fresh questions on Tuesday about his 2024 re-election bid, with one calling for him to step aside, a shift after many defended him in the wake of last week's shaky debate performance.\n\nATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nAtlantic City Beer and Music Festival canceled Nine rescued from waters off Ninth Street beach in Ocean City Wildwood: No more 'dog days' on the Boardwalk this summer Former site for Sister Jean's, 'Nucky' Johnson wedding to be demolished to make way for cannabis business New Jersey shut down the popular Remedee Coffee in Atlantic City Chicken Guy! restaurant debuts Tuesday in Atlantic City Water main break repaired in Wildwood Cape May Carriage horse dies on the job North Wildwood police located man in crisis they were searching for Rain can't keep thousands away from Wildwood beach soccer tournament Two arrested in undercover theft operation on Atlantic City boardwalk After an influential career, Mike Gatley steps down as Mainland Regional athletic director Galloway woman charged with 43 counts of neglect after multiple dead animals found at home Reduced hours for Ocean City Coast Guard station EHT graduates wear military honor cords: Hometown briefs\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates\n\nThat old age question (again)\n\nGetting personal\n\nBlunders without words\n\nGaffes galore\n\nBiden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
