{"uri": "8207812859", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:54:06", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:54:06Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:53:48Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.886274516582489, "url": "https://journalstar.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_8ed79330-5b1d-5e02-9d52-80b549fc0a84.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... North Lincoln grain elevator will soon come down after it was purchased at auction Crete, Nebraska: 7 shot in 'welcoming community' by neighbor who 'hated everybody' Lincoln in flood warning, more severe storms possible Tuesday afternoon 6 people injured in Crete shooting; suspect dead, State Patrol says 74-year-old identified as gunman in Crete shooting that injured 7 Garner Industries relocates in Lincoln after tornado destroys plant DJ Diesel, aka Shaq, draws superstar-sized crowd to Lincoln nightclub 'Let us in' -- Concertgoers voice frustrations after large crowd packed Royal Grove to see Shaq Rehab, reset: Jordy Bahl readying for second take with Nebraska softball Baker's Candies store in Greenwood to double in size with multimillion-dollar expansion Lincoln's Uncle Sam Jam celebration will now include food vendors America's first official 'quiet trail' sits in the Nebraska Sandhills Winning lottery ticket worth $22,000 sold in Lincoln Drivers will face nighttime detours on I-80 west of Lincoln this week Heavy rain causes flash flooding in Lincoln\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207789335", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:39:56", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:39:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:39:34Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8784313797950745, "url": "https://buffalonews.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_d9f0bc0d-01db-577a-9600-7bbe7e2c1ba8.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... A Buffalo teacher thought he chatted with a teen online. He got a surprise when he went to meet her Foxglove Pub in Hamburg closes its doors: 'The business just wasn't for us' Mike Harrington: Sabres are (naturally) thrilled with another first-rounder, but what about the deals? Inside Clarence's new million-dollar home development Sabres acquire fourth-line winger Beck Malenstyn for second-round draft pick Mike Harrington: Kevyn Adams maintains offseason is 'incomplete.' So what happens after Skinner buyout? Casey Mittelstadt's contract in Colorado may be a sign of Sabres' plan at center Erie County real estate transactions Long discussed, the DL&W is poised to become waterfront 'anchor' Sabres notes: GM Kevyn Adams trading back in first round could lead to bigger move Sabres begin process to walk away from Jeff Skinner's contract with three years left At fraud trial, Buffalo debt collectors describe their scripts threatening arrests: 'We told debtors we were coming' Buffalo Diocese's lawyers want $150,000 for legal work to keep sex abuse records secret Niagara Falls firm sues Hotel Niagara developer for breach of contract Hamburg resident dies in crash on Niagara Thruway in Buffalo\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "2024-07-410022938", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:34:42", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:34:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8784313797950745, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_ccbb44e4-95f6-5645-96b0-f36da4393fc3.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "Some elected Democrats loyal to President Joe Biden raised fresh questions on Tuesday about his 2024 re-election bid, with one calling for him to step aside, a shift after many defended him in the wake of last week's shaky debate performance.\n\nATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nOkoboji lakes remain at near record levels; water activities still curtailed as July 4 holiday nears Panel looks to release more water from Okoboji lakes without hurting flood-stricken Spencer, Cherokee Sergeant Bluff-Luton assistant principal, AD resigns days after being placed on administrative leave Where to go for firework displays in Siouxland McCook Lake faces questions of flood recovery, future mitigation Dairy plant sues Sanborn over loss of $4 million in cheese What to know before you go to Saturday in the Park 2024 Siouxland homes without flood insurance face coverage limits and costly repairs after flooding McCook Lake residents voice their flooding concerns to Sen. Mike Rounds OUR OPINION: Noem erred by not having National Guard help with S.D. flooding Cherokee residents try to stave off despair by jumping into action after flood Rock Valley police chief confirms flood-related death Man accused of vandalizing 12 vehicles in Sioux City parking ramp Million gallons of sewage flowing into Missouri River per day from Sioux City wastewater plant High school baseball top performers: East's Colling, RSM's Loucks, MOC's McCarty drive teams' push\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates\n\nGaffes galore\n\nGetting personal\n\nBlunders without words\n\nBiden-Trump redux\n\nThat old age question (again)\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nNorth Lincoln grain elevator will soon come down after it was purchased at auction Crete, Nebraska: 7 shot in 'welcoming community' by neighbor who 'hated everybody' Lincoln in flood warning, more severe storms possible Tuesday afternoon 6 people injured in Crete shooting; suspect dead, State Patrol says 74-year-old identified as gunman in Crete shooting that injured 7 Garner Industries relocates in Lincoln after tornado destroys plant DJ Diesel, aka Shaq, draws superstar-sized crowd to Lincoln nightclub 'Let us in' -- Concertgoers voice frustrations after large crowd packed Royal Grove to see Shaq Rehab, reset: Jordy Bahl readying for second take with Nebraska softball Baker's Candies store in Greenwood to double in size with multimillion-dollar expansion Lincoln's Uncle Sam Jam celebration will now include food vendors America's first official 'quiet trail' sits in the Nebraska Sandhills Winning lottery ticket worth $22,000 sold in Lincoln Drivers will face nighttime detours on I-80 west of Lincoln this week Heavy rain causes flash flooding in Lincoln\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates\n\nThat old age question (again)\n\nGaffes galore\n\nGetting personal\n\nBlunders without words\n\nBiden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207775388", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:31:07", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:31:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:30:38Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8745098114013672, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_69de7fad-111c-5606-a785-20c3c0ee1f8f.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Plans underway for Jimmy Carter's 100th birthday Man found dead near Lake James in McDowell County Man dies after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine; driver charged McDowell Sheriff's Office searching for missing woman McDowell man on probation charged with meth possession Marion man faces drug charges following search of home Rotary Club of Marion installs new officers, presents annual awards Marion man charged with fentanyl possession When are the 4th of July fireworks, parades in McDowell County? Abby Wyatt, three others from McDowell softball earn All-State honors West Marion project to revamp former school gets technical, fundraising aid Video: Ultralight crashes near Morganton after hitting tree McDowell Titans release 2024 varsity football schedule McDowell Children's Theatre presents kids musical Man arrested for stealing vehicle, wallet of man who died in Old Fort\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nRamsay's Kitchen by Gordon Ramsay is worse than bad. Column: Janet Jackson surprises St. Louis dance class, and I was there Work stops at Chesterfield music production facility after clash between builder, owner Groom shot at his wedding reception in south St. Louis remains in critical condition Missouri governor declares emergency as Missouri River swells St. Louis-area woman drowned in Gulf of Mexico, the fifth victim in four days at Florida beach St. Louis County man charged in drug ring has personal, campaign ties to state legislator Call for 'militia' at Ascension Catholic Church in Chesterfield was a mistake, pastor says Priest says call for militia has 'tainted' Ascension Catholic Church in Chesterfield State to exit downtown St. Louis by selling 2 office buildings Police move quickly to clear anti-Boeing protesters who blocked St. Louis' Pride parade St. Louis institution O'Connell's Pub is for sale Boeing moves forward with 'advanced combat aircraft' site in St. Louis County St. Louis sports-talk radio station KFNS plans to drop local programming: Media Views Collapsed mine in Alton was an active limestone operation. Workers evacuated in time.\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8207779694", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:33:42", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:33:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:33:16Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8549019694328308, "url": "https://statesville.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_5faa9f0e-c671-5c3e-ba52-30ca08943be7.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Iredell chase ends with crash; one reported dead Statesville planning board OKs development with at least 900 homes Former Greyhound Stevenson eager to play again for Wake Forest after recovering from ACL tear Statesville motorcyclist killed in crash near Mooresville Wednesday Iredell EMS deputy director talks about his termination. Appeal set for July 10 Harwell picked again to be umpire in charge for Babe Ruth World Series games US Marshals nab Statesville man wanted in fatal April shooting Greenville man struck, killed by tractor-trailer in Iredell County North Carolina Public health restaurant and food-stand inspections in Iredell County, June 14-27 Troutman Independence Day Parade starts holiday early Iredell County to host meeting regarding proposed new convenience center Iredell Health honors long-serving employees: Keith Williams started career at IMH and hopes to end it there Mooresville man convicted of murder at 14 suing state official over denied parole Statesville's Outstanding Teen captures state crown Man dies after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine; driver charged\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Getting personal Blunders without words Biden-Trump redux\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8207724491", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:01:01", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:01:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T12:00:45Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://theeagle.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/ominous-history-for-biden-incumbents-trying-to-win-over-their-parties-struggle-to-win-again/article_3836dbb7-1042-589d-9f86-dfd1a23e6e43.html", "title": "Ominous history for Biden: Incumbents trying to win over their parties struggle to win again", "body": "ATLANTA -- There's plenty of worry among Democrats about whether 81-year-old President Joe Biden is up to the job itself or the task of defeating Donald Trump.\n\nPrevious presidential campaigns offer lessons. None convey reasons for optimism.\n\nGoing back to Lyndon B. Johnson in 1968, several presidents eligible for reelection faced significant primary challenges or questions about whether they should run again. George H.W. Bush, Jimmy Carter and Gerald Ford pushed forward and won their nominations, only to be defeated in November. Johnson opted to withdraw -- and Democrats lost anyway.\n\nBiden had no real primary fight. But his allies now acknowledge how poorly the president performed in his debate against Trump. They've fretted privately about Biden's ability to serve until he is 86, and, more immediately, whether he can keep the job by defeating the Republican former president -- himself a 78-year-old saddled with a felony conviction, other indictments and voter concerns over his values and temperament.\n\nPeople are also reading... Texas A&M hires Washington head baseball coach Jason Kelly as pitching coach From players to possible coaching hires: What lies ahead in Texas A&M baseball's future? Even without college coaches, Texas A&M baseball camps must go on Baseball coach Jim Schlossnagle explains why he left A&M for rival Texas Watch: Jace LaViolette explains decision to stay at Texas A&M Bryan child dies in lightning strike, family starts GoFundMe Texas A&M AD Trev Alberts details days leading up to Jim Schlossnagle's departure Texas A&M hires Michael Earley as head baseball coach Bryan family settles into new house from Habitat for Humanity Three injured in 7-vehicle crash that closes Texas 6 northbound New Texas A&M baseball coach Michael Earley can teach hitting, but relationships is where he thrives Cessna: Scorned Aggies need to make Schlossnagle regret his move Texas A&M Chancellor John Sharp announces he will retire in a year Texas A&M baseball team set to compete in 2025 Astros Foundation College Classic Texas A&M baseball team announces annual team awards\n\nThe warning from history is ominous: Incumbent presidents still working to consolidate and reassure their own party this late in a first term typically do not get a second.\n\nGeorge H.W. Bush and the 'culture war' of 1992\n\nAn Ivy League-educated Episcopalian, Bush was a moderate Republican and never a favorite of the Christian right or anti-tax, small-government activists.\n\nBush appealed to the right flank ahead of his victory in 1988, saying, \"Read my lips: no new taxes.\" He was riding high in 1990 after a quick U.S. military victory drove Iraq and Saddam Hussein from oil-rich Kuwait. Within months, though, Bush broke his tax pledge, the U.S. economy began to falter (albeit mildly in retrospect), and the president grew vulnerable.\n\nPrimary challengers emerged, notably Steve Forbes, an anti-tax crusader, and commentator Pat Buchanan, a Christian conservative. Bush won every primary but many by unimpressive margins. Buchanan, rather than endorsing Bush enthusiastically, used his GOP convention speech to enlist religious conservatives in a \"culture war\" against Clinton, liberals and secularism -- standard Republican rhetoric today but a more divisive tone alongside Bush's talk of a \"kinder, gentler\" nation.\n\nDemocratic challenger and Arkansas Gov. Bill Clinton hammered Bush as out of touch with middle-class Americans. And billionaire Ross Perot entered the contest as an independent.\n\nOn Election Day, 62.6% of voters opted against Bush. Clinton won 370 electoral votes, the second-highest total for any Democrat since 1964.\n\nJimmy Carter and the Kennedy 'dream' in 1980\n\nA former Georgia governor, Carter was a moderate Southerner from outside the liberal Democratic power structure. His 1976 nomination and eventual victory over the Republican incumbent Ford was less about ideology, though, and more about Carter's promise never to lie to Americans disillusioned after Vietnam and the Watergate scandal.\n\nLegislative successes followed, but Carter rankled Washington Democrats. Global inflation, U.S. unemployment and interest rates climbed, and Carter's popularity fell.\n\n\"Carter was never expected and accepted by the establishment,\" said Joe Trippi, a 1980 Kennedy campaign staffer.\n\nSen. Ted Kennedy mounted a primary challenge in 1980, inspiring young progressives like those who had once adored his slain older brothers. Carter famously said of Kennedy, \"I'll kick his ass.\" The president won enough delegates for the nomination, even as the Iran hostage crisis compounded his problems.\n\nYet in defeat, Kennedy used his convention speech more to rouse his own supporters rather than reconcile with the incumbent. \"The work goes on, the cause endures ... and the dream shall never die,\" Kennedy declared, exposing Carter's weaknesses.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAgainst Republican Ronald Reagan, Carter carried just six states and Washington, D.C.\n\nGerald Ford and the budding Reagan revolution in 1976\n\nReagan won two general election landslides, but the foundation was his 1976 primary challenge against Ford.\n\nA mild-mannered Michigander, Ford had a unique path to the White House. President Richard Nixon elevated him from House leadership to the vice presidency in 1973 after corruption forced Spiro Agnew's resignation. Ford ascended to the presidency a year later when Nixon resigned because of Watergate.\n\nControversially, Ford pardoned Nixon. He faced inflation, high unemployment and roiled energy markets. And he had to prepare quickly to seek his own election, never having been part of a national campaign.\n\nFord hailed from Capitol Hill's center-right, a Republican cohort that mostly accepted the federal government's expanded scope since Franklin Delano Roosevelt's New Deal. Reagan, meanwhile, was corralling conservatives who never embraced FDR's America and blanched at the Civil Rights Movement and social revolutions of the 1960s and 1970s.\n\nIn the '76 primaries, Ford won 27 contests to Reagan's 24. That gave the incumbent 1,121 delegates, just 43 more than the insurgent challenger. Reagan had dominated most primaries in the South, the most conservative region of the country.\n\nIn the fall campaign, a wounded Ford made a late comeback against Carter but fell short. Carter carried the South. And Reagan was positioned to take the Republican mantle four years later.\n\nWhen a president did step aside: LBJ and 1968\n\nFord, Carter and Bush are not perfect parallels for 2024: Biden did not draw a credible primary challenge and, even with the debate fallout, he has a well of personal goodwill across his party. Perhaps the best comparison, then, is Johnson.\n\nThe assassination of John F. Kennedy thrust Johnson into the Oval Office in November 1963. Known as LBJ, the colorful Texan trounced Republican Barry Goldwater in 1964. Johnson amassed the most sweeping legislative record since FDR: the Civil Rights Act of 1964, Voting Rights Act of 1965, Medicare and Medicaid. But Johnson vastly expanded U.S. involvement in Vietnam -- and lied to the country in the process. He also found himself unable to shepherd Americans through social changes of the era.\n\nPresidential campaigns were shorter then, so it was not until March 31, 1968, that Johnson mulled his sagging standing and announced his intentions. After weak showings in early primaries, which were not then binding affairs, Johnson said in an Oval Office address, \"I shall not seek, and I will not accept, the nomination of my party for another term as your president.\"\n\nWhat followed, though, is not necessarily encouraging for Democrats hoping to hear the same from Biden.\n\nNew York Sen. Robert F. Kennedy -- whose son, Robert F. Kennedy Jr., is mounting an independent presidential bid this year -- joined a spirited Democratic nominating fight and secured momentum by winning the California primary in June. But he was assassinated in Los Angeles minutes after his victory speech.\n\nDemocrats were left with a raucous convention in Chicago -- also the site of the 2024 convention. They chose Vice President Hubert Humphrey to take on Nixon, the Republican former vice president who had lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960 and then dropped the 1962 California governor's race.\n\nNeither Nixon nor Humphrey were broadly popular, and the resulting general election was close, with independent George Wallace a key factor. Nixon outpaced Humphrey by about 500,000 votes out of 73 million cast, and he secured 301 electoral votes.\n\nSeven months after a beleaguered Democratic president stood down, his party met defeat. Republicans, with a president-elect who would one day resign in disgrace, had their comeback story.\n\nLaugh (or cringe) at these history-making moments from presidential debates That old age question (again) Gaffes galore Biden-Trump redux Getting personal Blunders without words\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
